        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595"><meta property="og:title" content="Effect of Local Atomic Structure on Sodium Ion Storage in Hard Amorphous Carbon"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/acs.nanolett.1c01595.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="The fundamental understanding of sodium storage mechanisms in amorphous carbon is essential to develop high-performance anode materials for sodium-ion batteries. However, the intrinsic relation between the structure of amorphous carbon and Na+ storage remains to be debated due to the difficulty in controlling and characterizing the local atomic configurations of amorphous carbon. Here we report quantitative measurements of Na+ storage in a low-temperature dealloyed hard carbon with a tunable local structure from completely disordered micropores to gradually increased graphitic order domains. The structure-capacity-potential correlation not only verifies the disputing “adsorption–intercalation” mechanisms, i.e., Na+ intercalation into local graphitic domains for the low-voltage plateaus and adsorption in fully disordered carbon for the sloping voltage profiles, but also unveils a new mechanism of Na+ adsorption on defective sites of graphitic carbon in the medium-potential sloping region. The quantitative investigations provide essential insights into the reaction mechanisms of Na+ with amorphous carbon for designing advanced sodium-ion battery anodes."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/acs.nanolett.1c01595.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187e758a80075b-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187e758a80075b-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '1e6136ef-4a36-4194-8ee8-72efe403077c'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

        });</script><style type="text/css">.MathJax_Hover_Frame {border-radius: .25em; -webkit-border-radius: .25em; -moz-border-radius: .25em; -khtml-border-radius: .25em; box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -webkit-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -moz-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; -khtml-box-shadow: 0px 0px 15px #83A; border: 1px solid #A6D ! important; display: inline-block; position: absolute}

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187e758a80075b-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="93187e758a80075b-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Effect of Local Atomic Structure on Sodium Ion Storage in Hard Amorphous Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Effect of Local Atomic Structure on Sodium Ion Storage in Hard Amorphous Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Effect of Local Atomic Structure on Sodium Ion Storage in Hard Amorphous Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Effect of Local Atomic Structure on Sodium Ion Storage in Hard Amorphous Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Effect of Local Atomic Structure on Sodium Ion Storage in Hard Amorphous Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Effect of Local Atomic Structure on Sodium Ion Storage in Hard Amorphous Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Effect of Local Atomic Structure on Sodium Ion Storage in Hard Amorphous Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Effect of Local Atomic Structure on Sodium Ion Storage in Hard Amorphous Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Effect of Local Atomic Structure on Sodium Ion Storage in Hard Amorphous Carbon", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="loa"><li><span><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor" tabindex="0" data-id="article_author_info" property="author">Jiuhui Han</span><div class="loa-info hlFld-Affiliation"><div Japan</div></div><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Jiuhui++Han" data-id="article_author_search">Jiuhui Han</a></span></div><div However, the intrinsic relation between the structure of amorphous carbon and Na<sup>+</sup> storage remains to be debated due to the difficulty in controlling and characterizing the local atomic configurations of amorphous carbon. Here we report quantitative measurements of Na<sup>+</sup> storage in a low-temperature dealloyed hard carbon with a tunable local structure from completely disordered micropores to gradually increased graphitic order domains. The structure-capacity-potential correlation not only verifies the disputing “adsorption–intercalation” mechanisms, i.e., Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into local graphitic domains for the low-voltage plateaus and adsorption in fully disordered carbon for the sloping voltage profiles, but also unveils a new mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption on defective sites of graphitic carbon in the medium-potential sloping region. The quantitative investigations provide essential insights into the reaction mechanisms of Na<sup>+</sup> with amorphous carbon for designing advanced sodium-ion battery anodes.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D6504%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJiuhui%2BHan%252C%2BIsaac%2BJohnson%252C%2BZhen%2BLu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D21%26issueNum%3D15%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.1c01595%26title%3DEffect%2Bof%2BLocal%2BAtomic%2BStructure%2Bon%2BSodium%2BIon%2BStorage%2Bin%2BHard%2BAmorphous%2BCarbon%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D6510%26publicationDate%3DAugust%2B2021" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290958&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Adsorption"> Adsorption </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291337&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon"> Carbon </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291445&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Chemical structure"> Chemical structure </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291064&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Intercalation"> Intercalation </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Amorphous+Materials&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_amorphous%20materials">amorphous materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Hard+Carbon&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_hard%20carbon">hard carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Local+Order&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_local%20order">local order</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Storage&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20storage">sodium storage</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><p class="first">As a promising alternative to current lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have received considerable attention because of the abundant supply of sodium and their similar electrochemistry to LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1−4)</a> Hard carbon is one of the most promising candidates for SIB anodes due to the abundant active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 5 to 9">(5−9)</a> It has been demonstrated that a large reversible capacity from 250 to 350 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> can be obtained from hard carbon. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref8" aria-label="reference 6 and 8">(6,8)</a> However, the mechanisms of sodium storage in hard amorphous carbon remain to be debated because of the technical challenges in controlling and characterizing the atomic structure of the amorphous materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 10 to 19">(10−19)</a> However, as the general consensus of all proposed structural models, hard carbon is composed of two mixed domains, i.e., graphitic-like local order and micropores formed by disorderly stacked carbon atoms, which correspond to the variation of capacity-potential profiles with distinguished sloping and plateau regions during Na<sup>+</sup> (de)insertion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref8" aria-label="reference 1 and 8">(1,8)</a> It has been suggested that the sloping region originates from defect-assisted Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation in graphitic layers and the plateau region from ion adsorption or filling in micropores, i.e., an “intercalation–adsorption” mechanism. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 6, 20 and 21">(6,20,21)</a> Oppositely, an “adsorption–intercalation” mechanism suggests that Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation occurs during the low voltage plateau while the sloping region results from pseudoadsorption at defective carbon sites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 22 to 24">(22−24)</a> Such uncertainty of the structural origins makes it difficult to understand the kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> storage in amorphous carbon. Herein, using a low-temperature dealloyed hard carbon with a versatilely designable atomic structure, we quantitatively investigated the effect of graphitic order and disorderly stacked micropores on sodium ion storage in amorphous carbon. The quantitative measurements of the relation between structure and capacity/potential unveil three Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites with different electrochemical kinetics in hard carbon.</p><p>The hard amorphous carbon (a-C) was synthesized by chemically dealloying a mesoporous Ni<sub>3</sub>C at room temperature (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a> in the Supporting Information). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Upon leaching Ni from the metastable carbide, the amorphous carbon was obtained, consisting of abundant and uniformly distributed micropores (pore size ∼ 0.55 nm) on the primary ligaments of the mesoporous structure (pore size ∼ 28 nm) (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). The resultant a-C sheets are flexible and freestanding. Due to the high chemical activity, the as-prepared carbon material contains abundant adsorbates of O- and Cl-containing functional groups (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Thermal treatment of a-C at high temperatures (&gt;650 °C) under Ar for 1 h can effectively remove those adsorbates and resume the high electrical conductivity of carbon (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). Meanwhile, the atomic structure of a-C can be exquisitely tailored by annealing (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>).</p><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/medium/nl1c01595_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/large/nl1c01595_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic Illustrations of the Hard Amorphous Carbon (a-C) and the Evolution of Its Atomic Structure after the Thermal Treatments under an Argon Atmosphere<sup>a</sup></div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/large/nl1c01595_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div><p class="last"></p><p class="last"><span class="fn-label"><sup>a</sup></span>The bottom left is an optical image showing the flexible and free-standing a-C sheet.</p><p></p></figure><p>We first examine structural features of a-C samples annealed under Ar atmosphere at the temperature of 900 °C (denoted as a-C-900). At this moderate temperature, the a-C well preserves the as-dealloyed microporosity. The scanning transmission electron microscope (STEM) image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a presents the bicontinuous mesoporous structure of a-C-900. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b shows the microporosity of the primary ligaments. Interestingly, local graphite-like segments, which are a typical structural feature of conventional hard carbon materials, cannot be seen here, indicating the creation of uniform and well-defined 3D microporosity has regulated a highly disordered structure of a-C-900. This disordered atomic structure is further evidenced by Raman spectroscopy and X-ray diffraction (XRD) measurements. The Raman spectrum shows broad D and G bands with a high areal intensity ratio (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) of 2.76 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). The XRD pattern also shows a very broad and weak diffraction peak located at 22.33°, corresponding to the (002) peak of graphite with an equivalent interlayer spacing of 0.398 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). This value is not only substantially larger than that of graphite (0.335 nm) but also surpasses those of most hard carbons (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>), suggesting a poorly developed local order in a-C-900. For quantitative analysis, the apparent average length along lateral <i>ab</i> direction (<i>L</i><sub>a</sub>) of potential graphitic carbon domains is calculated from the Raman data according to the following equation <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"></span></p><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/medium/nl1c01595_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i3"></div><div class="label"></div></div>where λ<sub>nm</sub> is the laser wavelength, 514.5 nm. The average length along the <i>c</i> direction (<i>L</i><sub>c</sub>) is estimated from the XRD result using the Scherrer equation. <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> and <i>L</i><sub>c</sub> are determined to be 6.10 and 0.96 nm, respectively, for a-C-900 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). Both values are much smaller than those of typical hard and soft carbon materials <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 24 and 27 to 30">(24,27−30)</a> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>g), demonstrating that the a-C-900 has a high level of structural disordering. More detailed comparisons are provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>. Combined with the electrochemical results showing plateau-free discharging/charging profiles which are to be discussed hereafter, we conclude that the a-C-900 could have a fully disordered carbon structure absent of local graphitic domains, presenting a unique structural feature that distinguishes it from conventional hard and soft carbon materials. As additional evidence for the complete structure disorder, the nitrogen- and sulfur-doped a-C samples are absent of dopant-induced graphitic interlayer expansions (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), which, in contrast, are typically observed in conventional N- and S-doped amorphous carbons. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 22, 31 and 32">(22,31,32)</a> Such highly disordered structure of a-C-900 should originate from the topological constraints arising from the microporosity that restricts the development of graphitic carbon at moderate temperatures.<p></p><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/medium/nl1c01595_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/large/nl1c01595_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Characterization of the a-C materials thermally treated under an Ar atmosphere at various temperatures. (a) High-angle annular dark-field STEM (HAADF-STEM) image of a-C-900. (b–d) HRTEM images of a-C-900, a-C-1300, and a-C-1800. (e) Raman spectra, (f) XRD patterns, and (g) the <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> and <i>L</i><sub>c</sub> for a-C-900, a-C-1300, and a-C-1800.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/large/nl1c01595_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>At the annealing temperatures of 1300 and 1800 °C, the formation of local graphitic carbon is apparent (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>), though both a-C-1300 and a-C-1800 retain the overall amorphous structure and the primary mesoporous structure is nearly unchanged (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e,f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). The HRTEM images show the coexistence of disordered and graphitic carbons while the graphitic order becomes more significant with the increase of the annealing temperature, as revealed by the increased volume fraction of graphitic carbon and the larger lateral coherence length and number of aligned graphitic layers (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c,d). These structural changes are endorsed by gas-sorption measurements showing the decreased surface areas for a-C-1300 and a-C-1800 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). Consistent with the enhanced local graphitic order, the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> drops to 2.24 and 1.30 for a-C-1300 and a-C-1800 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). The equivalent interlayer spacing of the graphite-like ordered structure is reduced to 0.391 and 0.379 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). The a-C-1300 and a-C-1800 also have larger <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> and <i>L</i><sub>c</sub> values (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>g), in good agreement with the HRTEM observations. Note that the <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> and <i>L</i><sub>c</sub> of a-C-1800 fall in the range of typical hard carbons. The physical parameters of the a-C samples are summarized in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>.</p><p>The a-C-900, a-C-1300, and a-C-1800 sheets were tested as self-supported electrodes in Na-ion half cells. The galvanostatic discharge–charge profiles reveal very different electrochemical behaviors of the three a-C electrodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a–c). At the low current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the a-C-900 shows plateau-free sloping discharge–charge curves, while the a-C-1300 has an obvious plateau at voltages lower than 0.1 V together with sloping curves at the higher voltage range. The plateau becomes even longer for a-C-1800. The discharge–charge characteristics of a-C-1800 are similar to those of conventional hard carbon electrodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref8" aria-label="reference 6 and 8">(6,8)</a> By correlating these electrochemical behaviors to the atomic structures of the a-C materials, it is clear that the low-voltage plateau is closely related to the local disorder-to-order structural transition and originates from the formation of the local graphitic domains. The discharge–charge behavior of the a-C-900 with disorderly stacked carbon atoms also verifies that Na<sup>+</sup> storage in the disordered micropores is characterized by sloping voltage profiles. In addition, the rich microporosity of the a-C-900, whereas the absence of plateaus, provides compelling evidence that the mechanism of attributing the plateau region to metallic Na filling/plating in micropores <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 6, 20 and 21">(6,20,21)</a> does not stand. There is a well-defined correlation of the sloping and plateau region capacities with the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio (an indication of structural disorder) and the BET surface area of a-C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). This evidences that the ‘disorder versus order’ atomic configurations regulate the sloping and plateau characteristics of amorphous carbon. Therefore, our results suggest that the flat low-voltage plateau should be attributed to the reactions in the locally ordered graphitic carbon and the storage of Na ions in disordered carbon is responsible, at least in part, for the sloping voltage profile.</p><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/medium/nl1c01595_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/large/nl1c01595_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Effect of carbon atomic structure on the sodium ion storage behavior and kinetics of a-C. (a–c) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles at various current densities for a-C-900, a-C-1300, and a-C-1800. (d) CV curves collected at 0.2 and 2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. The normalized current <i>i</i>* = current (<i>i</i>)/sweep rate (<i>v</i>). (e, f) Log (<i>i</i>) versus log(<i>v</i>) for the anodic current at 1.0 V (e) and peak current in the anodic scan (f) of the CVs. The <i>b</i>-values can be determined from the slopes of linear fittings according to the power-law relationship <i>i</i> = a<i>v</i><sup>b</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/large/nl1c01595_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>Such interpretation of the potential-capacity response is consistent with the “adsorption–intercalation” mechanism of Na<sup>+</sup> storage in amorphous carbon, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 22 to 24">(22−24)</a> i.e., the sloping voltage profile results from Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption on the disordered or defective sites that have a wide adsorption energy distribution while the low-voltage plateau originates from the formation of NaC<sub><i>x</i></sub> compounds by the intercalation reaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and locally ordered graphitic layers. To verify such electrochemical reactions, we conducted electrochemical kinetics analyses with the a-C electrodes. Generally, Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption is a surface-controlled reaction, while the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation in the confined graphitic interlayers features diffusion-limited kinetics. We examine the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves collected at different sweep rates and scale the current by the sweep rate (<i>i</i>* = <i>i</i>/<i>v</i>) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). For fast surface-controlled processes, the scaled current is rate-independent in a wide sweep rate range while, for ion-diffusion-limited Na<sup>+</sup> storage, the electrode current density drops with increasing rates as <i>i</i>* ∝ <i>v</i><sup>–1/2</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Consistent with the low-voltage plateaus in discharge–charge curves, the CV curves of a-C-1300 and a-C-1800 depict a pair of strong redox peaks at a voltage &lt; 0.1 V. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>, we observe decent scaling of the current for a-C-900 in the entire potential range and for a-C-1300 and a-C-1800 at potentials &gt;0.1 V, suggesting reactions at the high potential range are dominantly surface-controlled, in good agreement with Na<sup>+</sup> adsorptions. In contrast, the scaled current of the sharp peaks at the low-voltage region decreases apparently with increasing sweep rate, indicative of an ion-diffusion-limited process of charge storage, i.e., the characteristic of Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation reactions. Further quantitative analyses of the CVs based on the power-law relation of <i>i</i> = <i>av</i><sup><i>b</i></sup>, where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are constant, show <i>b</i>-values close to 1.0 for the anodic currents at 1.0 V and <i>b</i>-values close to 0.5 for peak currents in the anodic scan, also suggesting diffusion-controlled kinetics for the redox peaks and surface-controlled reactions of the high-potential regions (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e,f). Therefore, the kinetics analyses also support the above “adsorption–intercalation” mechanism.</p><p>As a-C-900 represents the fully disordered carbon while a-C-1300 and a-C-1800 have a mixture of disorderly microporous carbon and graphitic carbon, we then quantify the capacity contribution from disordered carbon in a-C-1300 and a-C-1800 by using a-C-900 as a benchmark, under the assumption that the disordered carbon components have the identical electrochemical behavior in these three electrodes. As the sloping and plateau regions are more distinguishable in the charging curves, the charging profiles are converted into the d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plots for this analysis (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). The plot of a-C-900 has a quasi-rectangular shape, consistent with the quasi-capacitive adsorption/desorption of Na<sup>+</sup> in disordered carbon. The d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plots of a-C-1300 and a-C-1800 show sharp peaks at the low-potential region that correspond to Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation in the graphitic domains. At the same current density, the d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plot of a-C-900 is weighted by a constant μ and then fitted to that of a-C-1300 or a-C-1800. The μ is optimized until the weighted plot of a-C-900 overlaps with the d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plot of a-C-1300 or a-C-1800 at the high potential region (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). The μ obtained by this method is actually the mass fraction of the disordered carbon component in a-C-1300 or a-C-1800, and the region beneath the weighted d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plot shows charge contributions from the disordered carbon. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b, the μ values are almost identical for current densities from 0.1 to 20 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and the mass fractions of disordered carbon in a-C-1300 and a-C-1800 are determined to be 63.6% and 36.7%, respectively. Similar values are also obtained by the analyses using the CV curves (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). These mass fractions are in good agreement with the HRTEM observations (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c,d). Interestingly, the sum of charge contributions from disordered-carbon-based Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption/desorption (the hatched areas in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a) and graphitic-carbon-based Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation (the peak areas at &lt;0.1 V in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a) cannot fulfill the whole Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity of both a-C-1300 and a-C-1800. There are remaining capacities corresponding to the bumps in the medium voltage range of 0.1–1.8 V, which should originate from a different Na<sup>+</sup> storage environment and is most likely from the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorptions on defective sites of graphitic carbon, such as the edges and in-plane defects of the rumpled graphene-like sheets. The medium voltage range for this reaction is also consistent with the fact that defective structures on graphite carbon are less random than fully disordered carbon and hence the reaction spans over a relatively narrower adsorption energy distribution. Note that the midvoltage capacity cannot be explained by the mechanism of “nanopore filling” since Na adsorption/filling in nanopores has lower binding energies and thus lower electrode voltages than the intercalation mechanism. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a></p><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/medium/nl1c01595_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/large/nl1c01595_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Discrimination of different Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms. (a) Derivative d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plots of the charging process at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> with the hatched portion showing charge contributions from the fully disordered carbon component. (b) Mass fractions of the disordered carbon component for a-C-1300 and a-C-1800. (c) Schematic illustration of the three Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms [(I) Na<sup>+</sup> intercalations in local graphitic domains; (II) Na<sup>+</sup> adsorptions on defective sites of the graphitic domains; (III) Na<sup>+</sup> adsorptions in fully disordered carbon)] and their corresponding potential regions.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/large/nl1c01595_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>Therefore, the Na<sup>+</sup> storage in amorphous carbon consists of three main mechanisms (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c): I, Na<sup>+</sup> intercalations in local graphitic domains corresponding to the low-voltage plateau region and having ion-diffusion-limited kinetics; II, Na<sup>+</sup> adsorptions on defective sites of the graphitic domains that give rise to part of the sloping curves at the medium potential range; and III, Na<sup>+</sup> adsorptions in fully disordered carbon that contribute to the entire sloping regions. Both II and III are dominantly surface-controlled reactions.</p><p>These mechanisms of Na<sup>+</sup> storage with different kinetics lead to divergent rate capability of the carbon structures. Among the three electrodes, a-C-900 shows the best rate performance. As the mass/volume fraction of ordered graphitic structures increases, the rate performance of the a-C-1300 and a-C-1800 electrodes gradually deteriorates (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a). At 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the a-C-900, a-C-1300, and a-C-1800 electrodes deliver the specific capacities of 246, 220, and 205 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (corresponding to the formation of NaC<sub>9.08</sub>, NaC<sub>10.15</sub>, NaC<sub>10.90</sub>), respectively. At 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacities drop to 137, 104, and 57 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to the capacity retention ratios (<i>C</i><sub>10&nbsp;A/g</sub>/<i>C</i><sub>0.1&nbsp;A/g</sub>) of 55.7%, 47.3%, and 27.9%. The series of discharge–charge profiles in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a-c show that the low-voltage plateaus are only apparent at low current densities while the sloping regions have a much weaker dependence on the reaction rates. According to the differentiated Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c), we quantitatively discriminate the capacity contributions from the three mechanisms (denoted as I, II, and III as detailed above) for the a-C electrodes. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b, the a-C-900 electrode is solely based on mechanism III, i.e., Na<sup>+</sup> adsorptions in fully disordered carbon. For a-C-1300 at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity contributions from mechanisms III, II, and I are 153, 44, and 23 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, accounting for 70%, 20%, and 10% of overall capacity (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). For a-C-1800, the capacity contributions and ratios are determined to be 89 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (43%), 41 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (20%), and 75 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (37%) for mechanisms III, II, and I (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). The a-C-1800 has a higher ratio of capacity contribution from mechanism I than II (37% versus 20%) as compared to that of a-C-1300 (10% versus 20%). This is consistent with the fact that the graphitic domains in a-C-1800 have a much larger size (such as larger <i>L</i><sub>a</sub> and <i>L</i><sub>c</sub>, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>g) and higher structural order (such as smaller interlayer spacing, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f) than those in a-C-1300, leading to less defective sites for Na<sup>+</sup> adsorptions and more interlayer sites for Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation. Remarkably, the charge contributions of the three mechanisms show dramatically different rate capabilities. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d, the rate behaviors of mechanism III are similar in all the three electrodes (<i>C</i><sub>10&nbsp;A/g</sub>/<i>C</i><sub>0.1&nbsp;A/g</sub> = 56–61%) and are much higher than that of the mechanisms II and I, suggesting the Na<sup>+</sup> storage in the disordered carbon has the fastest kinetics. Mechanism II in a-C-1800 shows poorer rate performance than mechanism II in a-C-1300, likely because the density of defective sites in the relatively large graphitic domains in a-C-1800 is lower and hence afford slower kinetics for Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption. Mechanism I displays the poorest rate performance among the three mechanisms, in good agreement with the ion-diffusion-limited kinetics for the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation in local graphitic domains. The rate performance of the mechanism I in a-C-1800 is also worse than that in a-C-1300, as the graphitic carbon in a-C-1800 has a large domain size and smaller interlayer spacing that deteriorate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity.</p><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/medium/nl1c01595_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/large/nl1c01595_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Capacity contributions and rate performances of the three Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms. (a) Overall capacity as a function of current density for a-C-900, a-C-1300, and a-C-1800. (b) The discriminated capacities from the three Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms (I, II, and III, refer to the caption of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>) as a function of current density. (c) The discriminated capacity contributions and ratios at the current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Capacity retentions with increasing current densities for the three Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/large/nl1c01595_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The unveiled mechanisms and kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> storage in hard carbon have important implications in understanding the fundamental Na-ion electrochemistry. The conventional “adsorption–intercalation” mechanism only considers the dichotomous mechanisms of Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption and Na<sup>+</sup> intercalations, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref24" aria-label="reference 22 and 24">(22,24)</a> while we found that Na<sup>+</sup> storage in hard carbon can be differentiated into three mechanisms which originate from the local ordered and disordered structures. Since the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorptions in disordered carbon are much faster than both the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalations and the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorptions in graphitic carbon, to achieve high rate capabilities, it is necessary to increase the degree of structural disorder in amorphous carbon materials. As demonstrated by the low-temperature dealloyed hard carbon, porosity engineering at subnm scale could be an effective strategy to regulate the structure of amorphous carbon materials for increasing atomic disorder. Though the sloping charge/discharge characteristics of highly disordered carbon would lead to high average electrode potentials (e.g., ∼1.2 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na for the a-C-900) and hence relatively low cell voltages, the “high”-potential anode is particularly beneficial for high-rate applications as it minimizes the risk of dendrite formation on the anodes for high safety. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> It is also necessary to note that highly disordered/microporous structures are usually accompanied by large surface areas, leading to more aggressive solid electrolyte interface (SEI) reactions for a low initial Coulombic efficiency (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>). Such Coulombic efficiency issue should be considered when exploiting this structural design.</p><p class="last">In summary, we’ve quantitatively investigated the effect of local atomic structure on sodium ion storage in amorphous carbon using an exquisitely designed model material with tunable atomic structures from disordered micropores to gradually increased local graphitic order. The quantitative study uncovers the complete picture of Na<sup>+</sup> storage in hard carbon, consisting of three main mechanisms: Na<sup>+</sup> intercalations in local graphitic domains corresponding to the low-voltage plateau region in charge/discharge curves, Na<sup>+</sup> adsorptions on defective sites of graphitic domains for part of the medium-potential sloping region, and Na<sup>+</sup> adsorptions in fully disordered micropores that span over the entire potential range with a sloping characteristic. Na<sup>+</sup> storage in disordered carbon has the fastest kinetics as compared to the others. Porosity engineering at the subnm scale is effective in regulating the local structures of amorphous carbon for both large capacities and outstanding rate capability.</p><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Experimental procedures, further XPS, Raman, XRD, TEM characterizations, CV analyses, and tables of the physical parameters for the materials (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf">nl1c01595_si_001.pdf (1.54 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research development on sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Chem.
