Future outlook of sodium-ion batteries is discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201410376"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kundu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Talaie, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Duffort, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nazar, L.                 10.1002/anie.201410376</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D54%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D3431-3448%26journal%3DAngew.%2BChem.%252C%2BInt.%2BEd.%26author%3DD.%2BKundu%26author%3DE.%2BTalaie%26author%3DV.%2BDuffort%26author%3DL.%2BF.%2BNazar%26title%3DThe%2Bemerging%2Bchemistry%2Bof%2Bsodium%2Bion%2Bbatteries%2Bfor%2Belectrochemical%2Benergy%2Bstorage%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fanie.201410376&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.1c01595&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fanie.201410376&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of some of the current scientific issues underpinning sodium-ion batteries.  Cathode and anode materials and electrolytes for high-performance sodium-ion batteries as well as computational studies of sodium ion mobility are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201800212"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Exploration of advanced electrode materials for rechargeable sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent advances in titanium-based electrode materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Guo, Shaohua; Yi, Jin; Sun, Yang; Zhou, Haoshen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2978-3006</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, the attention to sodium-ion batteries has been refocused on large-scale energy storage applications, due to sodium's low cost and infinite abundance.  Sodium is one of the most abundant elements on earth and exhibits chem. properties similar to lithium.  Owing to their superior sodium storage capability esp. for excellent safety and stability, Ti-based compds. have been extensively investigated as both cathode and anode materials.  Herein we outline the current cathodes and anodes, and emphasize the crit. roles of titanium in developing advanced electrodes for sodium-ion batteries.  The latest advances and progress in the exploration of Ti-based compds. with various different frameworks such as NASICON, tunnel, MXenes, spinel, and layered structures are systematically reviewed.  The straightforward linking of the structure-function-property relationship for Ti-based compds., esp. for layered Na/Ti-contg. oxides, is summarized and analyzed.  The titanium element plays a crit. role in both pos. and neg. electrodes, i.e., supplying the charge transfer and high safety for anodes and greatly enhancing the structural and cycling stability for cathodes.  Based on the bi-functional roles of titanium, a new concept of sym. Na-ion cells employing layered Na/Ti-contg. oxides as bipolar electrodes is proposed and realized.  Sym. Na-ion cells already offered a high voltage and withstood long time charge-discharge processes, demonstrating the practicality beyond the proof of concept.  The participation of titanium in sodium-based electrode materials will greatly promote the development of room-temp. sodium-ion batteries towards stationary energy storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1149/1.1379565"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stevens, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahn, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The mechanisms of lithium and sodium insertion in carbon materials</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors compare the interactions of lithium and sodium with a range of carbon materials in electrochem. cells.  Through wide angle in situ x-ray scattering studies, both lithium and sodium can be inserted into the interlayer space in disordered carbon materials.  This insertion process is accompanied by an increase in the interlayer spacing in these materials.  Small-angle in situ scattering studies are presented to clearly show the insertion of lithium and sodium into nanopores within disordered hard carbons.  Also very little, if any, sodium can be inserted into graphitic materials in contrast to the large capacity seen for lithium insertion.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1002/smtd.201800227"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Z.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have dominated the energy storage market for more than two decades; however, the quest for lower-cost battery alternatives is rapidly expanding, esp. for large-scale applications.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have recently experienced an impressive resurgence owing to the earth's abundance of sodium resources and the similar electrochem. of SIBs and the well-established LIBs.  Nonetheless, whereas cost-effective and reliable graphite anodes have served as a cornerstone in current LIB technol., one of the major limitations of SIBs has been the inability to exploit graphite as an electrode because of its negligible sodium storage capability.  Recently, however, clear progress has been made in prepg. high-performance graphitic carbon anodes for SIBs with new findings on the mechanisms of sodium storage.  Herein, this paper aims to review the progress made in understanding the sodium storage mechanisms in graphitic carbon materials and comprehensively summarize the start-of-the-art achievements by surveying the correlations among the type of graphitic material, microstructure, sodium storage mechanisms, and electrochem. performance in SIBs. E.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Crystallite growth in graphitizing and non-graphitizing carbons</span>. <i>Proc. R. Soc. London, Ser. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1951</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>209</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">196</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">218</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1098/rspa.1951.0197</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D209%26publication_year%3D1951%26pages%3D196-218%26journal%3DProc.%2BR.%2BSoc.%2BLondon%252C%2BSer.%2BA%26author%3DR.%2BE.%2BFranklin%26title%3DCrystallite%2Bgrowth%2Bin%2Bgraphitizing%2Band%2Bnon-graphitizing%2Bcarbons%26doi%3D10.1098%252Frspa.1951.0197&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.1c01595&amp;doiOfLink=10.1098%2Frspa.1951.0197&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Crystallite growth in graphitizing and nongraphitizing carbons</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Franklin, Rosalind E.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Proceedings of the Royal Society of London, Series A: Mathematical, Physical and Engineering Sciences</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1951</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">209</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">196-218</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PRLAAZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1364-5021</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">cf. C.A. 45, 6895d.  An investigation of the structure of carbons of different origin treated at temps. between 1000 and 3000° showed that the graphitizing and non-graphitizing carbons form 2 distinct and well-defined classes.  The differences in structure are apparent from the earliest stages of carbonization, and can be attributed mainly to the formation at low temps., in the nongraphitizing carbons, of a strong system of cross-linking uniting the crystallites.  This leads to a random orientation of the crystallites in a rigid, finely porous mass.  In the graphitizing carbons the cross-linking is much weaker, the structure is more compact, and neighboring crystallites have a strong tendency to lie in nearly parallel orientation.  Carbons are obtained from polyvinylidene chloride (com. product Saran), sugar charcoal, hexachlorobenzene, coal of 82.4% C (Northumberland), coal of 83.1% carbon (Yorkshire), polyvinyl chloride, petroleum coke, pitch coke, and Welsh coking coal.  The non-graphitizing carbons are formed, in general, from substances contg. little H or much O.  On heating such substances there develops, at low temps., a strong system of cross-linking which immobilizes the structure and unites the crystallites in a rigid mass.  The resulting carbons are hard, and their fine-structure porosity is large and is preserved at high temps.  The nongraphitizability of these carbons results from their porous structure and the random orientation of the crystallites.  The graphitizing carbons are prepd., in general, from substances contg. much H.  The crystallites remain relatively mobile during the early stages of carbonization, and cross-linking in the mass is weak.  The carbons are softer and less porous than the non-graphitic carbons prepd. under the same conditions, and their crystallites are more highly developed, especially in the direction perpendicular to the layers.  The anthracites, at temps. below 2000°, form strongly cross-linked, highly porous carbons that greatly resemble the non-graphitizing carbons.  They differ from the latter, however, in that they show a strong preferential orientation of the crystallites in the mass.  In order that crystallite growth and graphitization should occur readily in a given C, two essential conditions must be fulfilled.  The system of cross-linking which unites the crystallites in the mass must not be too strong, and neighboring crystallites must lie in near-parallel orientation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1038/231175a0"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jenkins, G. M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kawamura, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Structure of glassy carbon</span>. <i>Nature</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1971</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>231</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">175</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">176</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/231175a0</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D231%26publication_year%3D1971%26pages%3D175-176%26journal%3DNature%26author%3DG.%2BM.%2BJenkins%26author%3DK.%2BKawamura%26title%3DStructure%2Bof%2Bglassy%2Bcarbon%26doi%3D10.1038%252F231175a0&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.1c01595&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2F231175a0&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structure of glassy carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jenkins, G. M.; Kawamura, K.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature (London, United Kingdom)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1971</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">231</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5299</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">175-6</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NATUAS</span>;

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Glassy carbon, prepd. by the pyrolysis of phenolic resins, has a network structure consisting of tangled aromatic ribbon mols. which are cross-linked by highly strained C-C bonds with a wide spectrum of bond energies.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Microporous and activated carbons have numerous applications ranging from gas absorbers to liq. decolorizers and are often called carbon mol. sieves.  Their properties depend critically on the size of their pores and pore openings.  Their structures are very complex and have defied a description which can explain the structural changes which occur during the thermal or chem. treatments necessary to adjust their properties.  Here, we present a simple qual. model, the "falling cards model", which describes how the pore size and structure of these important materials changes with processing conditions.  Comparison to expt. shows that the model works well. The shape of these nanosized voids is characterized by a combination of ellipsoid and random branching forms.  The lithiation proceeded by 2 steps.  At a shallow lithiation region, Li intercalated into the periodically layered region and the interlayer spacing of this region increased.  At the deep lithiation stage, Li was inserted into the interlayer nanovoids and void size increased.  The coordination nos. of the constituent ions indicated a 2-dimensional graphene sheet in the whole structure - this supports the wave model for the hard C structure.  Based on the x-ray and n scattering expts. a new structure model and lithiation mechanism are proposed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1149/1.1394081"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stevens, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">An electrochem. cell with beryllium X-ray windows has been designed and used for in situ small-angle X-ray-scattering studies of operating electrodes for the first time.  This cell is ideally suited to the study of the filling of nanoscopic pores in solids by electrochem. transported atoms.  The mechanism of electrochem. lithium and sodium insertion in nanoporous carbonaceous materials has been the subject of some recent controversy, which is resolved by the studies reported here.  We show indisputable evidence that the filling of the pores accounts for all the sodium (and lithium) inserted into these carbons at a chem. potential near that of metallic sodium (or lithium).  At lower chem. potential (higher cell voltage), sodium (or lithium) is inserted between graphene layers in an intercalation mechanism.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201100854"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Murata, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishikawa, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ozeki, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nakayama, T.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Na Insertion and Solid Electrolyte Interphase for Hard-Carbon Electrodes and Application to Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Nongraphitizable carbon, also known as hard carbon, is considered one of the most promising anodes for the emerging Na-ion batteries.  The current mechanistic understanding of Na-ion storage in hard carbon is based on the "card-house" model first raised in the early 2000s.  This model describes that Na-ion insertion occurs first through intercalation between graphene sheets in turbostratic nanodomains, followed by Na filling of the pores in the carbon structure.  We tried to test this model by tuning the sizes of turbostratic nanodomains but revealed a correlation between the structural defects and Na-ion storage.  Based on our exptl. data, we propose an alternative perspective for sodiation of hard carbon that consists of Na-ion storage at defect sites, by intercalation and last via pore-filling.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201700403"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sushko, M.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">3D Bimodal Porous Amorphous Carbon with Self-Similar Porosity by Low-Temperature Sequential Chemical Dealloying</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Jiuhui; Li, Hongping; Lu, Zhen; Huang, Gang; Johnson, Isaac; Watanabe, Kentaro; Chen, Mingwei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Synthesizing 3D porous carbon with at.-scale control in geometry and topol. of porous architectures is of great significance while it tech. remains challenging.  Dealloying, the selective dissoln. of less-stable elemental components from an alloy, is one of the most effective top-down approaches to fabricate 3D nanoporous materials for a wide range of functional applications.  Here, we report a sequential metastable-carbide-mediated chem. dealloying approach to fabricate 3D bimodal porous amorphous carbon that possesses geometrically well-defined and topol. self-similar meso- and microporosities.  The synthetic route allows independent and precise control of the bimodal porosity, by which micropores can be regulated at angstrom-scale accuracy, and mesopores can be tailored over a wide range of lengths from several nanometers to hundreds of nanometers.  The 3D bimodal porous amorphous carbon enables fast ion diffusion and hence delivers outstanding rate performance when used as the anodes for Na-ion storage.  This study not only offers a new method for the controllable synthesis of 3D porous carbon materials but also demonstrates the capability of dealloying as an advanced material-processing method to engineer the porous structure down to the angstrom level.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref26" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1063/1.2196057"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cançado, L.                         cas_cit27R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">27</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Slope-Dominated Carbon Anode with High Specific Capacity and Superior Rate Capability for High Safety Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Qi, Yuruo; Lu, Yaxiang; Ding, Feixiang; Zhang, Qiangqiang; Li, Hong; Huang, Xuejie; Chen, Liquan; Hu, Yong-Sheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The comprehensive performance of carbon anodes for Na-ion batteries (NIBs) is largely restricted by their inferior rate capability and safety issues.  Herein, a slope-dominated carbon anode is achieved at a low temp. of 800°C, which delivers a high reversible capacity of 263 mA h g-1 at 0.15C with an impressive initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 80%.  When paired with the NaNi1/3Fe1/3Mn1/3O2 cathode, the reversible capacity at 6C is still 75% of that at 0.15C, and 73% of the capacity is retained after 1000 cycles at 3C.  The enhanced Na storage performance could be attributed to the unique microstructure with randomly oriented short carbon layers and the relatively higher defect concn.  Given its robustness, such a low-temp. carbonization strategy could also be applicable to other precursors and provide a new opportunity to design slope-dominated carbon anodes for high safety, low-cost NIBs with excellent ICE and superior rate capability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref28" class="refNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit28" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201703238"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons have become recently one of the most promising classes of anode materials for Na ion batteries (NIBs) owing to their high specific capacity and good cycling stability.  Among the precursors used to prep. hard C, phenolic resins are of great interest due to their high C yield, however, their toxicity must be overcome.  The authors propose a green, simple and scalable procedure to obtain phenolic resins which by pyrolysis at high temp. (&gt;1000°) result in eco-friendly hard carbons with low surface area, disordered structure and high C yield.  The influence of several synthesis parameters (type of solvent, thermopolymn./annealing temp. and gas flow) was studied to det. the impact on both phenolic resin and hard C characteristics.  The synthesis time (12 h-3 days) depends on the used solvent whereas the C yield (25-35%) on the crosslinking degree which could be controlled by adjusting both thermopolymn. temp. and atm.  The structure of the hard carbons mainly changed with the carbonization temp. (1100-1700°) while the texture of the material was sensitive to most of the studied parameters.  Stable reversible capacity up to 270 mA h g-1 and 100% coulombic efficiency (CE) after few cycles are obtained, demonstrating the potential for Na-ion applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1039/C7TA01394A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kiso, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Horiba, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chafik, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hida, T.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis of hard carbon from argan shells for Na-ion batteries</span>. <i>J. H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Templated nanocrystal-based porous TiO<sub>2</sub> films for next-generation electrochemical capacitors</span>. <i>J. Am. Chem. Soc.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2009</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>131</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1802</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1809</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Templated Nanocrystal-Based Porous TiO2 Films for Next-Generation Electrochemical Capacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Brezesinski, Torsten; Wang, John; Polleux, Julien; Dunn, Bruce; Tolbert, Sarah H.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2009</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">131</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1802-1809</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The advantages in using nanoscale materials for electrochem. energy storage are generally attributed to short diffusion path lengths for both electronic and lithium ion transport.  Here, another contribution, the charge storage from faradaic processes occurring at the surface, referred to as pseudocapacitive effect are considered.  This paper describes the synthesis and pseudocapacitive characteristics of block copolymer templated anatase TiO2 thin films synthesized using either sol-gel reagents or preformed nanocrystals as building blocks.  Both materials are highly cryst. and have large surface areas; however, the structure of the porosity is not identical.  The different titania systems were characterized by a combination of small- and wide-angle x-ray diffraction/scattering, combined with SEM imaging and physisorption measurements.  Following the authors' previously reported approach, the authors are able to use the voltammetric sweep rate dependence to det. quant. the capacitive contribution to the current response.  Considerable enhancement of the electrochem. properties results when the films are both made from nanocrystals and mesoporous.  Such materials show high levels of capacitive charge storage and high insertion capacities.  By contrast, when mesoscale porosity is created in a material with dense walls (rather than porous walls derived from the aggregation of nanocrystals), insertion capacities comparable to templated nanocrystal films can be achieved, but the capacitance is much lower.  The results presented here illustrate the importance of pseudocapacitive behavior that develops in high surface area mesoporous oxide films.  Such systems provide a new class of pseudocapacitive materials, which offer increased charge storage without compromising charge storage kinetics.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187e758a80075b-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

Schematic Illustrations of the Hard Amorphous Carbon (a-C) and the Evolution of Its Atomic Structure after the Thermal Treatments under an Argon Atmosphere<sup>a</sup></div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/large/nl1c01595_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div><p class="last"></p><p class="last"><span class="fn-label"><sup>a</sup></span>The bottom left is an optical image showing the flexible and free-standing a-C sheet.</p><p></p></figure><figure data-id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/medium/nl1c01595_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/asset/images/large/nl1c01595_0001.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr1" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1.                         rightTab-cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research development on sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Chem.                         rightTab-cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent advances in titanium-based electrode materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Guo, Shaohua; Yi, Jin; Sun, Yang; Zhou, Haoshen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, the attention to sodium-ion batteries has been refocused on large-scale energy storage applications, due to sodium's low cost and infinite abundance.  Sodium is one of the most abundant elements on earth and exhibits chem. properties similar to lithium.  Owing to their superior sodium storage capability esp. for excellent safety and stability, Ti-based compds. have been extensively investigated as both cathode and anode materials.  Herein we outline the current cathodes and anodes, and emphasize the crit. roles of titanium in developing advanced electrodes for sodium-ion batteries.  The latest advances and progress in the exploration of Ti-based compds. with various different frameworks such as NASICON, tunnel, MXenes, spinel, and layered structures are systematically reviewed.  The straightforward linking of the structure-function-property relationship for Ti-based compds., esp. for layered Na/Ti-contg. oxides, is summarized and analyzed.  The titanium element plays a crit. role in both pos. and neg. electrodes, i.e., supplying the charge transfer and high safety for anodes and greatly enhancing the structural and cycling stability for cathodes.  Based on the bi-functional roles of titanium, a new concept of sym. Na-ion cells employing layered Na/Ti-contg. oxides as bipolar electrodes is proposed and realized.  Sym. Na-ion cells already offered a high voltage and withstood long time charge-discharge processes, demonstrating the practicality beyond the proof of concept.  The participation of titanium in sodium-based electrode materials will greatly promote the development of room-temp. sodium-ion batteries towards stationary energy storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1149/1.1379565"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stevens, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahn, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The mechanisms of lithium and sodium insertion in carbon materials</span>. <i>J. A.; Dahn, J. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors compare the interactions of lithium and sodium with a range of carbon materials in electrochem. cells.  Through wide angle in situ x-ray scattering studies, both lithium and sodium can be inserted into the interlayer space in disordered carbon materials.  This insertion process is accompanied by an increase in the interlayer spacing in these materials.  Small-angle in situ scattering studies are presented to clearly show the insertion of lithium and sodium into nanopores within disordered hard carbons.  Also very little, if any, sodium can be inserted into graphitic materials in contrast to the large capacity seen for lithium insertion.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1002/smtd.201800227"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Z. L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yoon, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Graphitic carbon materials for advanced sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Small Methods</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>3</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1800227</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/smtd.201800227</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Graphitic Carbon Materials for Advanced Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Zheng-Long; Park, Jooha; Yoon, Gabin; Kim, Haegyeom; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small Methods</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1800227</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have dominated the energy storage market for more than two decades; however, the quest for lower-cost battery alternatives is rapidly expanding, esp. for large-scale applications.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have recently experienced an impressive resurgence owing to the earth's abundance of sodium resources and the similar electrochem. of SIBs and the well-established LIBs.  Nonetheless, whereas cost-effective and reliable graphite anodes have served as a cornerstone in current LIB technol., one of the major limitations of SIBs has been the inability to exploit graphite as an electrode because of its negligible sodium storage capability.  Recently, however, clear progress has been made in prepg. high-performance graphitic carbon anodes for SIBs with new findings on the mechanisms of sodium storage.  Herein, this paper aims to review the progress made in understanding the sodium storage mechanisms in graphitic carbon materials and comprehensively summarize the start-of-the-art achievements by surveying the correlations among the type of graphitic material, microstructure, sodium storage mechanisms, and electrochem. performance in SIBs.  In addn., perspectives related to practical applications, including the electrolyte, coulombic efficiency, and applicability in sodium-ion full cells, are also presented.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201703268"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saurel, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Orayech, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Carriazo, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rojo, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">From charge storage mechanism to performance: A roadmap toward high specific energy sodium-ion batteries through carbon anode optimization</span>. <i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>8</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1703268</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

E.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Crystallite growth in graphitizing and non-graphitizing carbons</span>. <i>Proc. R. Soc. London, Ser. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1951</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>209</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">196</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">218</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1098/rspa.1951.0197</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Crystallite growth in graphitizing and nongraphitizing carbons</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Franklin, Rosalind E.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Proceedings of the Royal Society of London, Series A: Mathematical, Physical and Engineering Sciences</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1951</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">209</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">196-218</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">PRLAAZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1364-5021</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">cf. C.A. 45, 6895d.  An investigation of the structure of carbons of different origin treated at temps. between 1000 and 3000° showed that the graphitizing and non-graphitizing carbons form 2 distinct and well-defined classes.  The differences in structure are apparent from the earliest stages of carbonization, and can be attributed mainly to the formation at low temps., in the nongraphitizing carbons, of a strong system of cross-linking uniting the crystallites.  This leads to a random orientation of the crystallites in a rigid, finely porous mass.  In the graphitizing carbons the cross-linking is much weaker, the structure is more compact, and neighboring crystallites have a strong tendency to lie in nearly parallel orientation.  Carbons are obtained from polyvinylidene chloride (com. product Saran), sugar charcoal, hexachlorobenzene, coal of 82.4% C (Northumberland), coal of 83.1% carbon (Yorkshire), polyvinyl chloride, petroleum coke, pitch coke, and Welsh coking coal.  The non-graphitizing carbons are formed, in general, from substances contg. little H or much O.  On heating such substances there develops, at low temps., a strong system of cross-linking which immobilizes the structure and unites the crystallites in a rigid mass.  The resulting carbons are hard, and their fine-structure porosity is large and is preserved at high temps.  The nongraphitizability of these carbons results from their porous structure and the random orientation of the crystallites.  The graphitizing carbons are prepd., in general, from substances contg. much H.  The crystallites remain relatively mobile during the early stages of carbonization, and cross-linking in the mass is weak.  The carbons are softer and less porous than the non-graphitic carbons prepd. under the same conditions, and their crystallites are more highly developed, especially in the direction perpendicular to the layers.  The anthracites, at temps. below 2000°, form strongly cross-linked, highly porous carbons that greatly resemble the non-graphitizing carbons.  They differ from the latter, however, in that they show a strong preferential orientation of the crystallites in the mass.  In order that crystallite growth and graphitization should occur readily in a given C, two essential conditions must be fulfilled.  The system of cross-linking which unites the crystallites in the mass must not be too strong, and neighboring crystallites must lie in near-parallel orientation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref11" class="rightTabRefNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit11" data-doi="10.1038/231175a0"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jenkins, G.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Microporous and activated carbons have numerous applications ranging from gas absorbers to liq. decolorizers and are often called carbon mol. sieves. Their structures are very complex and have defied a description which can explain the structural changes which occur during the thermal or chem. treatments necessary to adjust their properties.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The structure and lithiation mechanism of non-graphitizable C (hard C) fiber were detd. by x-ray and n diffraction-pattern simulation, n small-angle scattering, and n total scattering techniques.  The structure of the hard C is formed by 2-dimensional graphene sheets, which have a narrow interlayer spacing and slightly larger interlayer spacing, corresponding to a periodically layered region and interlayer voids, resp.  The shape of these nanosized voids is characterized by a combination of ellipsoid and random branching forms.  The lithiation proceeded by 2 steps.  At a shallow lithiation region, Li intercalated into the periodically layered region and the interlayer spacing of this region increased.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Nongraphitizable carbon, also known as hard carbon, is considered one of the most promising anodes for the emerging Na-ion batteries.  The current mechanistic understanding of Na-ion storage in hard carbon is based on the "card-house" model first raised in the early 2000s.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Synthesizing 3D porous carbon with at.-scale control in geometry and topol. of porous architectures is of great significance while it tech. remains challenging. Here, we report a sequential metastable-carbide-mediated chem. dealloying approach to fabricate 3D bimodal porous amorphous carbon that possesses geometrically well-defined and topol. self-similar meso- and microporosities.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbons have become recently one of the most promising classes of anode materials for Na ion batteries (NIBs) owing to their high specific capacity and good cycling stability.  Among the precursors used to prep. hard C, phenolic resins are of great interest due to their high C yield, however, their toxicity must be overcome.  The authors propose a green, simple and scalable procedure to obtain phenolic resins which by pyrolysis at high temp. (&gt;1000°) result in eco-friendly hard carbons with low surface area, disordered structure and high C yield.  The influence of several synthesis parameters (type of solvent, thermopolymn./annealing temp. and gas flow) was studied to det. the impact on both phenolic resin and hard C characteristics.  The synthesis time (12 h-3 days) depends on the used solvent whereas the C yield (25-35%) on the crosslinking degree which could be controlled by adjusting both thermopolymn. temp. and atm.  The structure of the hard carbons mainly changed with the carbonization temp. (1100-1700°) while the texture of the material was sensitive to most of the studied parameters.  Stable reversible capacity up to 270 mA h g-1 and 100% coulombic efficiency (CE) after few cycles are obtained, demonstrating the potential for Na-ion applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref30" class="rightTabRefNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit30" data-doi="10.1039/C7TA01394A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kiso, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Horiba, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chafik, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hida, T.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis of hard carbon from argan shells for Na-ion batteries</span>. <i>J. Mater. Chem. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">9917</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C7TA01394A</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis of hard carbon from argan shells for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Dahbi, Mouad; Kiso, Manami; Kubota, Kei; Horiba, Tatsuo; Chafik, Tarik; Hida, Kazuo; Matsuyama, Takashi; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9917-9928</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon is an attractive material for neg. electrodes in sodium-ion batteries.  Herein, we report a new hard carbon synthesized via carbonization of argan shell biomass, which delivers an enhanced capacity higher than 330 mA h g-1 based upon reversible sodium insertion.  We prepd. hard carbon under different high-temp. treatment and biomass pretreatment conditions.  The graphitization degree of the hard carbon increased as the carbonization temp. increased; simultaneously, the reversible capacity for sodium storage was significantly influenced by the carbonization temp.  Structural characterization revealed differences in the structures of the hard carbons synthesized at different carbonization temps., which elucidates the correlation between the increased capacity and the micropore size available for sodium storage.  The composite electrodes contg. the argan hard carbons with a sodium polyacrylate binder were tested in non-aq. sodium half cells.  The electrodes delivered reversible capacities as high as 300 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 25 mA g-1 with superior reversibility and capacity retention of 94.1% after 70 cycles.  By carbonization of argan shell biomass treated with HCl aq. soln., we successfully demonstrated a higher reversible capacity of 333 mA h g-1 and an excellent capacity retention of 96.0% after 100 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref31" class="rightTabRefNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit31" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201802035"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hong, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhen, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ruan, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J. M.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Rational design and general synthesis of S-doped hard carbon with tunable doping sites toward excellent Na-ion storage performance</span>. <i>Adv. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>30</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1802035</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adma.201802035</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref32"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref32" class="rightTabRefNumLink">32</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit32" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201604108"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">S-doped N-rich carbon nanosheets with expanded interlayer distance as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Adv. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>29</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1604108</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adma.201604108</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref33" class="rightTabRefNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit33" data-doi="10.1021/ja8057309"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Brezesinski, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Polleux, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dunn, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tolbert, S. H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Templated nanocrystal-based porous TiO<sub>2</sub> films for next-generation electrochemical capacitors</span>. <i>J. Am. Chem. Soc.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2009</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>131</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1802</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1809</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/ja8057309</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Templated Nanocrystal-Based Porous TiO2 Films for Next-Generation Electrochemical Capacitors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Brezesinski, Torsten; Wang, John; Polleux, Julien; Dunn, Bruce; Tolbert, Sarah H.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2009</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">131</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1802-1809</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The advantages in using nanoscale materials for electrochem. energy storage are generally attributed to short diffusion path lengths for both electronic and lithium ion transport.  Here, another contribution, the charge storage from faradaic processes occurring at the surface, referred to as pseudocapacitive effect are considered.  This paper describes the synthesis and pseudocapacitive characteristics of block copolymer templated anatase TiO2 thin films synthesized using either sol-gel reagents or preformed nanocrystals as building blocks.  Both materials are highly cryst. and have large surface areas; however, the structure of the porosity is not identical.  The different titania systems were characterized by a combination of small- and wide-angle x-ray diffraction/scattering, combined with SEM imaging and physisorption measurements.  Following the authors' previously reported approach, the authors are able to use the voltammetric sweep rate dependence to det. quant. the capacitive contribution to the current response.  Considerable enhancement of the electrochem. properties results when the films are both made from nanocrystals and mesoporous.  Such materials show high levels of capacitive charge storage and high insertion capacities.  By contrast, when mesoscale porosity is created in a material with dense walls (rather than porous walls derived from the aggregation of nanocrystals), insertion capacities comparable to templated nanocrystal films can be achieved, but the capacitance is much lower.  The results presented here illustrate the importance of pseudocapacitive behavior that develops in high surface area mesoporous oxide films.  Such systems provide a new class of pseudocapacitive materials, which offer increased charge storage without compromising charge storage kinetics.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i4">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Experimental procedures, further XPS, Raman, XRD, TEM characterizations, CV analyses, and tables of the physical parameters for the materials (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.1c01595/suppl_file/nl1c01595_si_001.pdf">nl1c01595_si_001.pdf (1.54 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	});

	});

<script nonce="93187e758a80075b-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();
          }
          //Events

            hideForm();
          });
        }, time);
      }

      //Show the message
      function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $("#gdpr-message-modal").show();
      }

      function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...
          setCookie("gdpr", "true");
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
          //Show error message
          gdprErrorMessage();
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
      }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;
        } else {
          errorStatus = true;
          $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
      }

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

    $(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
  </script>
</p>
-->

                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="93187e758a80075b-SJC">
var currentJournal = "Nano Letters";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>
